RATING: 8
I see a lot of potential in this game.  I've played two games so far, one with three players (thanks for suffering through it, Brad--I know it's not your kind of thing) and one with two. I'm pretty impressed with the newish Avalon-Hill/Hasboro games.  
[update]  So far, the two team attempts I've played haven't turned out that great.  I think the balance of experience and luck in these games was the culprit.  I still think it would make an excellent team game amongst well-balanced teams.

RATING: 6.6
Played a friend's copy and it's a nice, light little wargame. Hasbro tried to spiff it up with sort of glow in the dark pieces which made it look cheap to me. Some interesting tactical choices but little strategy. I'm not a fan of multi-player games in which a player can be elliminated like this one. The game probably works best with four players. It's pretty rough as a three player because two players can pinch the middle guy and it's game over for him. There's sort of a cute "King of the Hill" aspect too with the raised middle section. Overall, better than you'd think but just by a little.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A quick, light, and easy wargame! It's... INGENIOUS!!!

The pieces are certainly cool and nice to look at, though the box is a bit overly-yellow. The modular board is very nice, colorful, and cartoony. The game scales well for any number of players; the staggered starting income is a great balancing mechanism, and all the exploration tiles are roughly equal in value. I use blue acrylic crystals for rubium, which add a Starcraft-esque coolness to the game.

Also, the pieces have a distinct alien-odor that just adds to the theme! :p
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Finally got this to the table for a first play with a couple I met through BGG (Dick and Laurie).  It was a good learning session, and the game was a lot of fun and seems to be pretty deep for what it is.  

I had a lot of fun throwing away all of my units in a desperate bid for VP, falling just one short of the required 12 for victory (if one die roll had been a 4 instead of a 2 I would have pulled it off!) A turn later, Dick attacked my home base with three Rubium Dragons from the monolith.  He won the battle, and with two secret mission cards, the attack netted him 7 VP in one fell swoop, for a huge and surprising come-from-behind win!  He had been in last place with only 6 vp to my 11 and Laurie's 8 or 9!  Great game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Pure Ameri-Trash goodness!  

This game contains almost all that is happy in a single box.  For starters, it has garish artwork on the cover that looks like Napoleon Dynamite drew it.  That rocks.

It has plastic.  Mmmmm...plastic!  And that plastic glows under a dark light.  If anything is more cool than that, I'm taking nominations.  Anything?  Nope.  Didn't think so.

And the plastic: it has things called "rock crawlers", "rubium dragons", and "lava leapers", but you know what they REALLY are?  They are spiders, dragonflies, and dinosaurs.  Yeah, you heard me: this game has dinosaurs in it, and if you know anything about anything, you know that dinosaurs are the most awesome thing e-vah, and will increase the "kickassedness"  factor of any game by at least an order of magnitude.

What else does it have?  It has a cheap cardboard monolith, and if you can't have a plastic one, I want a cheap cardboard one!  Yeah!  That rules!

Best of all, it drips with theme, conflict, dice, crystalline forests, and much other goodness.

But I'm going to warn ya: if you are wuss or a Eurogamer (one in the same?), stay away from this game, because it will frighten you.  You will wake up screaming from the bad dreams this game will give you, because you have never stood face-to-face with this much awesomeness in your life.

This game plays equally well with 2, 3, or 4 players.  

Note: I'm talking about the original Hasbro version.  The re-made FFG version lacks ANY monolith and the figures do not glow under a black light.  That is weak.  Get the original if you can.

RATING: 6
A fun game but pretty random.  Combat is resolved by dice rolls, though more expensive units clearly have the advantage.  I love the glow-in-the-dark (well, not really) pieces!  Gameplay goes fairly quick and the game length is pretty good.

I would probably have rated it a 7+ except for the fact that scoring is pretty random.  It's entirely possible to be losing your battles and have the fewest pieces on the board, yet have the highest VP due to played mission cards.  Granted that's just part of the random element (which is pretty high in this game), and I'm not *that* big a fan of randomness, but I wish there was a bit more control and/or a more direct or tighter connection between success on the board and winning.

I would definitely play Nexus Ops again, though it wouldn't be my first choice.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Fortunately, my luck changed by the end of the year. I found some great flatmates who became my new gaming group. So now we usually play in three or four players. As they are new to boardgaming I let them choose what we'll play, so not to impose my own taste on them.
One of the games that they requested was Nexus Ops. Nexus Ops was a game I didn't warm much to. It is a great introductory game because people usually love the translucent bits. The table talk it generates creates a great social atmosphere. And the dice keep it from taking itself too seriously. But I found it a little bit too fiddly with all the bits and moving them on the modular board, and space consuming with all the cards spread around. Also, I had the feeling that luck of the draw of the mission cards influenced the winner too much. But after playing the game again I changed my mind. I realized that the key to play Nexus Ops well was being aggressive and discriminatory with the mission cards you draw. If the mission cards are unachievable discard them for rubidium and the ones you keep fulfill as soon as possible. With this simple observation the game was no longer playing me, but the other way around. There is still the possibility that you can have a run of extremely bad luck and lose for a point because you drew worse mission cards than your opponents, but now in those situations I feel ecstatic that I could offer them a worthy resistance and not just waiting for the game to finally finish, as I did before.
Sometimes it's good to give games a second chance.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Nexus Ops is a fun, risk-like game that my friends and I have enjoyed immensely! The first few times we played the games lasted over an hour and a half. We couldn't comprehend how other players were knocking out games in half an hour to fourty-five minutes.

I then played it with another group of friends. Unlike my original game group, these friends did things such as attack hexes with only two or three units built up. They used humans as fodder, but didn't need twelve of them stacked up before they felt it was safe to attack. Astonishingly, we played two games and had a smoke break in the time it took my original group to play one.

The problem is that my original group played very defensively. There must have been a group-think problem as well because we all played that way. I'm sure they will enjoy the game much more with an aggressive play style. I know I do.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A great (and more fun) alternative for those who are fans of Risk.  Still a lot of dice throwing and luck during battles, but lots more decisions to be made, based on different terrain types, resources, and opponents troop types.

One great thing about this is that it is difficult to get knocked out of the game, since the farther someone stretches from their home base, the weaker they will get.  By the time they get to your home base, their troops are significantly spread out and can be held off.  (You will still lose if they are all the way to your base, but as least you can still effect the end game)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Very intuitive tactics game. I like skirmish games, variable unit types to deploy, income management, and randomized map setups. Nexus Ops offers a light approach to all of these mechanics (yay!), but the lightness means that there is a heavy degree of luck (boo!). Nonetheless, the heart of the game isn't the luck, it's the secret mission victory point system. 

The secret missions really make the game and help Nexus Ops have a laser focus on a being a quick sci-fi dudes on a map game with just a little bit of strategy. Just enough to keep it fun.

RATING: 6
Decent theming and some reasonable ideas, notably the secret mission cards that keep games short and aggressive, while varying your objectives a bit. The cute, glow in the dark pieces are nice too.  As with Vegas Showdown, the rules are exemplary.  Also as with Vegas Showdown, there's some obvious cost cutting (the monolith and the useless box divider) in an otherwise well produced package.   

Nexus Ops is a simple slugfest; there are none of the strategic and tactical subtleties that other simple wargames offer.  And a poor start, due to the luck of the discovery tile draw and/or some bad die rolls, will very quickly leave you trailing in the wake of other players.  Nonetheless, I had a bit of fun playing this game a couple times.

RATING: 7
Each aspect of the game's design is not unique, but the collection of mechanism makes for a convincing little game.  There is a large luck factor in both combat (i.e. dice) and in objectives (i.e. cards).  The latter is the larger drawback since a series of unusable or impractical objective cards will slow VP gains.  To the game's credit, this drawback is handled admirably well and results in a surprisingly well balanced game play.  Keep the game play action moving for best results.  [GTI Code: TPCL]

RATING: 8.1
Dudes on a map, but some pretty freaky alien dudes battling over resources for various science fiction institutions. Pretty straightforward area control and simplistic combat, with victory points coming from both your prowess in battle and specific objectives you'll try to achieve. I do wish the factions were a little more asymmetrical and had more personality, but you can use the Alternate Units on the back sides of the reference sheets, and there are quite a number of little variants you can employ into the game. A classic for a reason, and you can see how it inspired other designs, though I can't deny that it's been surpassed a number of times.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Like many wargames, can be a bit unbalanced with three players, where staying out of the initial fighting and letting the other two players weaken themselves can be key.  Fun and quick to learn.  This is not one of those games where you spend the whole first game figuring out how you should be playing.  In the A.H. version, the production values of the monolith in the middle is a little bit of a letdown, but the great plastic figures more than make up for this.  Who says cubes rule?  The sculpts of the Avalon edition are unique and wonderful.

A game with the emphasis on tactics rather than strategic decisions, not a lot of deep thinking involved.  Can see playing this occasionally, but not every game night.

RATING: 7
Warcraft, Solo

A nice short, light wargame with great bits (even if they do smell a little rubbery when you first open the box). There's not enough variety for me, though---there's only one scenario, and the game doesn't seem to be made to have more than one.  Each player has the same kind of army, which yields only six different units.  But I do like the event tiles for exploration, the secret mission cards, and the way you get energize cards.  The units that do exist are interesting to play with, and the landscape may not vary too much from game to game, but each terrain type definitely has its own effects.  But I don't think this is the RTS-like game for me, though I think it would be very enjoyable for many.  Kudos to AH/WotC/Hasbro here.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Some people like the old components such as units that glow under black light but I'm more than happy with the average looking tiles and very nicely sculpted units from FFG.

The battleground is randomly generated and you play for victory points, which come from achieving varied objectives. Area control is an element: you need to control the high value tiles. However, just as often you're attacking a different tile to complete a secret objective.

Combat is similar to Axis and Allies in that different units hit on different roles, but different because more powerful units attack before weaker ones.

RATING: 7
Sort of a grown-up version of Risk (bear with me). Quite dice heavy, you get more reinforcements if you have more territory and play is directed towards building up big armies and using them to rampage into opposing territory. That being said, the races are nicely differentiated and balanced, so that 3 or 4 of the weakest (humans) can gang up on and kill one of the most powerful (dragons). The tactics (go for a big area to get income or the monolith to get extra cards) seem nicely balanced also. Not the most mentally demanding of games but lots of fun with cool figures thrown in.

If you like chucking dice and killing troops this game is a must.

RATING: 6
Seems to combine some elements of Axis & Allies (unit purchases and aspects of combat from the D-Day game), RISK (Secret Mission and Energize cards), Titan (creature recruitment and funky hex board), and Chitin:I (weird creatures with different fighting abilities). The resulting game is a surprisingly quick and light wargame (90 minutes for our frst game) that rewards using Energize cards to leverage fulfilling the conditions of the Secret Mission cards. "Turtling" is probably the least viable strategy because you don't always have to win battles to earn victory points. But fighting IS the quickest path to victory, so mix it up!

RATING: 7
I think this would be an "8" for me a few years ago. Perhaps unfairly I am ranking it as a "7" perhaps because I've played and enjoyed so many games that imitated the best elements of Nexus Ops (secret objectives, building resource engines, unit special powers) in a more sophisticated, thematic way. "Lava leaper" just doesn't resonate for me, so sometimes the game's dice-iness seems a little naked. All of that said, this is really a very clean, very tight, super-light war game with a small footprint of investment, time, table space and rules complexity. I admire Nexus Ops and always enjoy a session. I usually opt for something a little heavier and more thematic (I'd probably go for Kemet, at least), but for playing with my friends with less tolerance for heavier games, it's absolutely perfect. I don't expect to ever let it leave my collection; there just too few games like this out there.

RATING: 7
Juego sencillo y entretenido. Se trata de un juego de toñas, que llama la atención por el material de las figurillas.. de una especie de plástico fluorescente muy llamativo. Es un juego de "todos contra todos". Cada jugador dispone de un ejército y el objetivo es conseguir 12 puntos de victoria. Se obtiene un punto de victoria por cada batalla que ganas. Alternativamente se pueden obtener puntos de victoria cumpliendo una serie de "misiones secretas".
Es un juego sencillito y sin grandes pretensiones. Es como una versión muy simplificada de wargames básicos, tipo Axis and allies. A su favor. Dura poco. La partida se resuelve en poco más de una hora.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Hands down the best wargame-lite on the scene. The board is made up of hexagonal regions placed in a hexagonal pattern which favors no player and offers the same opportunities and risks to all.

The mission cards give every player their own objectives that often encourage aggressive play. Units are purchased at the beginning of one's turn and reflect the price you pay for them (i.e. a more expensive unit will have a marked advantage over a cheaper unit) so the dice-based fighting system is properly balanced and doesn't cause infuriating situations where an ill-prepared player steamrolls a stronger opponent through lucky rolls.

Nexus Ops is generally much shorter than its counterparts which is also a huge positive in my opinion.

RATING: 4
I really tried to like Nexus Ops, and it has some cool stuff in it - the objective cards are a cool idea, even if not particularly well balanced. I think what kills the game here though is the economic subsystem. In a four-player game, waiting for everyone else to add up their credits and spend them on stuff really kills game momentum, and makes the downtime hard to tolerate. I enjoyed it well enough, but I think there is no reason to play this ahead of the two Star Wars Risk games, which I think really take the genre of Risk-like games and moves them forward, while most other similar games (this, Antike, Risk: Godstorm, Risk: 2210) have bolted-on economic games which detract from rather than add to the interest of the game. This whole genre is about sharp action and taking risks, not about economics, and I think the need to fiddle economic systems detract from the game. The decisions about troop mixes simply aren't that interesting, unlike in, say, Axis & Allies, where infantry/tank/air mixes in your forces are pretty important (A&A has other problems, of course).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is a great system for a light bashy combat game.

First off, the pacing and playtime are excellent. Even your first time through you'll be done in under 2 hours - which is just about how long my attention span lasts for any given game. I like the way that things heat up fast - maybe 2-3 turns of just exploration and building up units/currency, then, due to the pleasingly small map size, you're all up in each other's faces ready to start throwing down. Nice!

And fighting in this game WILL happen. This is not a game that encourages defensively play. This is a huge plus in my book. Too many "combat" games boil down to turtling.

I'm usually not a fan of secret objectives but they work well here because they're not long-term secrets nobody is going to know to how to prevent, they're just the kind of small things that come up through regular play and everyone should always be trying to do the same things anyway so preventing people from getting them is a matter of course.

The components are lovely. The creatures are weirdly cute and while I might never make use of the glow factor they LOOK super cool just because they can glow. The tiles have nice art too!

The energize cards are a great mechanic. I feel like in games with dicey combat, being rewarded for being beat up on is a MUST and these do the trick pretty well.

The king of the hill thing it's got going on in the center of the map is great in theory but can have its issues. Firstly, it feels like 2 energize cards per turn is too rich a reward because they just help you KEEP the monolith locked down.

There are a lot of ways in which luck affects the outcome, obviously. You can roll poorly despite your best efforts. You can have games where it's simply easier for one player to get to the monolith. You can have games where you're not getting energize cards or secret objectives that are helpful/achievable in your current situation, or one player can start near all the most lucrative mines. But overall I think the luck factor is acceptable for the game length.

On that note - the game works well with any number of players on the box but with the more you have, the more you can gang up on someone who's just getting lucky.

Also: I dig that the game ends immediately if one player is eliminated. More games need that rule.

Despite the tangible luck factor, I always have fun when I play this. Yeah, there's a lot of luck but sometimes you just want to roll some dice to beat some dudes in the face and this is a clean, elegant system for just that.

RATING: 8
This is a very nice mix of ameritrash and eurogame. You attack each other and destroy units, but you do so in order to complete secret objectives and be the first player to achieve 12 points, at which point you win immediately. I tend to like games in which you can win immediately and this is definitely no exception.

I love the way you can get points: by winning combats as the attacker and via secret objectives, which I’m always a sucker for. The secret objectives can be to destroy a specific unit and non-combat missions like controlling most forest areas etc. This is really awesome and what definitely makes the game shine for me! The slightly assymetric setup and especially the 6 different units also make the game very exciting. The units have special powers (and the units are “double-sided, meaning the variety is significantly increased), and while the dragon is awesome, you need to have some cannon fodder too due to how combat works (which is evidence of a smooth design).

Nexus Ops is a very good game, and while the Monolith is very strong indeed as you get two cards each round, so if a player gets a foothold and fortify the Monolith, the other player needs to be worried, although it’s definitely not a sure thing that you win if you control the Monolith, which has been proven in several of our games. The cards, the exploration bonuses, the units and the objective cards are what make Nexus Ops such a great game!

RATING: 8
Received this in a trade for Colosseum and a new copy of Thunderstone.

Initial rating an 8. First off, I'm glad I got this in a trade. If I spent approx. $75 for an out-of-print copy, I'd probably be kicking myself. Because I didn't break the bank, I can enjoy the game for what it is... a fun combat-themed dice fest with plenty of luck but also enough meat and tactics behind it.

The game is simple enough... spend rubium to make units, send units out to battle, win battles to score VPs. The fun is in mixing up the different units, racing to control the mines and gain those freebie units, and taking advantage of terrain. 

I admire my good gaming friends for their Heroscape collection... but Nexus Ops works just fine as a Cliff-note sort of version in a much smaller package. Fun little game that I look forward to trying with 2v2 teams.

Update: Still haven't played this game with a lot of regularity, but every time I do, I enjoy it. Finally got a chance to play the team version, and it was a blast. There's wasn't a whole lot we could do to really help each other, but some of that might have been the hex layout. Still, rooting for another player and passing the occasional card to help adds a nice element. Not a lot of games handle teams well, and this one does.

Update: having traded for the fantasy flight version of the game I can say that I like it slightly better. I don't care if the minis don't glow in the dark :) the tile artwork could be better but the slight variants are a nice add.

RATING: 8.5
Who needs Risk when you have this

small map +
varied units +
objectives +
short game length ++

when I want to play Risk (which is admittedly not vary often) I play this instead.

Update: Who needs Twilight Imperium when you can just play this. It seems that old Nexus Ops does a way better job trying to capture the spacey combat TI3 ever could ( and as a quick rebuttal to those who would disagree, if TI3 wasn't a combat game then why is the ENTIRE focus of the game (besides the objectives, which don't involve combat at all, paradoxically) revolve around ships and army men, getting resources (to build ships and army men), tech (to upgrade ships and army men), laws (to restrict/reward ships and army men) and on and on!)

Nexus Ops just does everything right, the combat, the high luck factor, varied objectives and perfect game length conspire to make this easily my favorite light wargame.

RATING: 5
The colours are quite garish, and your fate is very tightly tied to the overall feasibility of the special missions cards that you draw, but this is a fast playing Risk variant done just about right with just the right mix of complexity and speed. I really can never rate a game like this very highly because your ability to play it "well" is more or less your ability to roll the dice well and to play to your hand as well as possible (and to draw the right cards at the right time), but it's not a bad little game all told. The victory point mechanism as such is a very good idea as it makes the game finish out much faster than similar style games which alone makes this something I'm much more likely to sit down to. That said, the novelty for this game seems like it'll be short, and that the cards and situations will start to feel very samey with very many plays. I have to quibble about the random mine distribution as well, like Eagle's War it can be overly generous / stingy and that can really affect the chances of players because income is so very important. All of this said, the randomness of this game is relatively acceptable given the genre (American Light War Dice-Chucker) that plays out quickly, but it isn't enough to really engage me that much either.

RATING: 7
I played Memoir '44 on Thursday and my first game of Nexus Ops on Friday.  Nexus feels like a lighter version of Memoir with a SF theme, more randomness, and a Risk feel. I enjoy piling up units and rampaging across the countryside.  It was fun to watch each other save up Rubium to buy dragons and attempt to beat the crap out of each other.  The secret mission cards add an interesting twist but are hard to keep track of during play.  I "won"our game by getting two victory points for winning a battle on a forest hex, which seemed excessively generous, so I traded the card for Rubium instead and we continued to play, with the game coming down to a final battle consisting of my two dragons vs. my opponent's rockstrider and dragon.  He barely won the battle, beating me by one victory point, and we had one of those game box/tv commercial moments with loud shouts and fist pumping, etc. so that was cool.  My six year old played with us and did ok (we will give him a six point handicap next game) but I was too busy figuring the game out to help him as much as I should have.  He started off strong but then got greedy,following a mining strategy and hoarding dragons, which didn't get him a lot of points. A fun game!

RATING: 7.5
Played once so far, with 4 players. Great fun. 

Light combat game. Easy to learn. Nice miniatures. Unique (garish?) colors. A mix of units each with different costs, and different pros and cons. Felt a bit like a board game version of Starcraft, except everyone has identical units available to them. A fair amount of complexity. Game board will be different every time. The objective cards, special action cards, terrain effects, initial exploration tile goodies, and "king of the hill" element add to the fun.

The first couple turns were a land grab, but battle lines quickly stagnated. Then it was all about fighting lots of little battles for victory points. If you like sweeping across the board and gaining territory, this game isn't for you (at least with 4 players). Some gamers will dislike all the dice rolling.  Took longer than expected - about 2 hours I think. Sure it's derivative - but it's fun, so who cares.

RATING: 6
I love some things about this game - the "gain points by serving your corporate board's arbitrary goals" is both vastly entertaining and creates very different play than, eg, purely territorial or economic win conditions would.

Nonetheless, after about half a dozen games, it doesn't have a strong "want to keep playing" attraction. It might be a length-of-play issue, which often feels like it's a touch longer than it ought to be for the amount of stuff that actually happens. Might be solvable via speed play?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Dudes on a map.  Dudes that glow in black light, that is...

This game is simple and straight-forward. Expand so you can buy even more guy to expand even further.  Beat up on your opponents to gain VPs.  Win.

Playing to take over more of the map than your opponents won't get you a win.  Instead, you have to make sure you play according to your Secret Missions.  It can be difficult to reign yourself in sometimes, but there are occasions where 6 Humans are more important to buy late in the game than one Dragon.

This game plays pretty quick, and it scratches that Risk itch that you sometimes get.  

We'll see if it has staying power.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

Great fun all round.

A fast game with easy rules where interaction (battles) between players is impossible to avoid.

There is some confusion regarding the identification of some units (they are similar) and which units have moved or not in the same turn. The combat system can also get messy through the final stages of the game, where there are more troops involved.
My absolute debut failure in this game (for example, i didn´t realize at once that stronger units must be "escorted") don´t prevent me from concede that this is a game that provides a wide range of options and strategies. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
A decent Ameritrash, because it doesn't suffer from terribly long playtime.  The randomness of the dice is still frustrating.  Overall, there are some cool, unique mechanics and plenty of replayability.  I've only played 2-player so far.  I like how the various units are specialized and have their own functions and limitations.  Nice way to encourage combat with the Monolith.  It is difficult (as a new player) to keep track of and remember all the cards you have in your hand towards the middle of the game.  Finally, the bits are way cool (except for the cheap player reference cards)!

RATING: 7.5
(2013)
"Dudes on a map" per 2-4 jugadors i 120 min.
Amb 3 partides no ha cuajat: tot i les mecàniques per 'compensar' els que van perdent, quan algú es posa al capdavant, sembla que es manté i és impossible aturar-lo (almenys a 2 jugadors).
=====
Amb 4 partides (3 a 2 jugadors) jo començo a veure-li moltes, moltes possibilitats. Les diferents unitats, diferents iniciatives, el factor terreny, etc pot donar tàcticament molt de sí.
A 2 és necessari que els dos rivals tinguin nivell similar, sinó es desequilibra massa.
A 3 o 4, es pot fer front comú i mirar de frenar al que s'avanci massa.
Efectivament el joc premia l'atac, però cal anar amb compte i no atacar "per què sí" provocant que després també t'ataquin i ells també puntuin.
=====
Amb 6 partides, definitivament li trobo el punt. El problema és que tinc ventatja (per tenir més partides) sobre els demés, i l'experiència compta més del que sembla.
Es poden fer punts de moltes maneres.
Descartar cartes inútils a canvi de cristall no és mala opció.
En la darrera partida, els meus dos últims torns foren de 6 i 5 punts.
A 2 jugadors segueixo tenint la sensació del Runaway leader (tot i que el King of the Hill podria solventar-ho).
=====
Amb 14 partides, la majoria a 2...... ufff, el joc mola i promet molt, però hi ha un patent efecte "runnaway leader". Si tens més terreny i més unitats i controles el monolit i l'altre no te'l pot arrabassar, està sentenciat. La bola de neu es fa gran i gran i no hi ha forma humana de parar-ho. 
Males tirades de daus als primers torns poden sentenciar la partida, deixant-te en una inferioritat que no pots recuperar.
Potser cal incloure algun mecanisme de catch-up com en Tash-Kalar...
=====
Variants per millorar a 2:
1) https://boardgamegeek.com/thread/491328/2-player-monolith-mod
Durant els primers 6 torns (usar un dau per contar), el monolit només dóna 1 carta d'Energia. Després ja 2. Això fa que algú amb sort de pillar rockstriders ocupi el monolit, es reforci amb cartes d'energia i es desequilibri la partida.
2) Meva. Monolit no dóna cartes d'Energia, només King of the Hill. El problema d'això és que no hi hauria batalla pel monolit que és l'essència.
3) Meva. Monolit dóna 2 cartes d'Energia al "ocupador" i 1 a l'altre.
4) Algo per compensar males tirades. Pensar.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[12-20-2012 Rating: n.a. -> 7]
Nexus Op contains a lot of mechanics I normally don't like in games. You have direct conflict, randomly assigned action cards that let you break the rules or up your winning chances in a battle and a lot of dice rolls that can totally screw your plans. 

But somehow, Nexus Op manages to be fun for me. The game plays fast and normally, you are done with it within an hour. The gameplay is very tactical: It doesn't matter if you have lost the control of a tile or not as long as you can fulfill your secret mission cards. Sure, you can be hosted if you draw secret mission cards that you can't easily fulfill in your current situation, but coupled with the short playing time this isn't a problem for me. The whole game is about aggression, action efficiency and choosing your battles. Yeah, I would play this again.

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Players represent mining corporations trying to extract minerals from a planet, and beat up the competition.  A good old fashioned, die rollin', beat 'em up game.  Players take turns mining money, spending the money on troops, moving the troops, attacking other players, and hopefully fulfilling victory conditions.  A player wins if they can reach twelve victory points.  Victory points are earned if they win a battle (1) or achieve a secret mission - and this is the clever bit.  Secret missions are...secret, so even if you're being hammered into extinction, a clever build phase and a bold attack can still net you victory points.  Fast, simple, fun -  and the glowing miniatures don't hurt either.  A nice surprise.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game has a lot of things I love.  I love the graphic design, the modular board, the cool components, the strategic decision making, the units and their abilities, the win condition, etc. It also scales well from 2 to 4 players. The only complaint is that sometimes the secret mission cards can be too random. Sometimes you draw one that goes perfectly with your current position or is already accomplished, sometimes you draw one that you won't be able to achieve.

Edit: I sometimes forget the rule about discarding a card to get 1 Rubium. This somewhat balances the concern above, you can discard unattainable secret missions to get more rubium.

RATING: 7
Good basic dice-rolling combat game, if a bit generic-feeling.  A huge advantage is that it is one of the very few dice-rolling multiplayer wargames that is playable within about 2 hours or less.  Secret mission card victory points are fun, if somewhat too random sometimes-- your success greatly depends on these missions being easy for you to complete.  The most common way the game bogs down is if someone uses a defensive strategy against you and gathers a ton of cheap units in a hex on your border, which are impossible to eliminate in one combat round-- so that you can't complete most missions or get VPs.

Here are links to a couple of sources selling cool-looking UV-A shades for about $20:  http://www.blacklightworld.com/UV_Glasses.htm or http://www.blacklight.com/items/98000201.  Goggles like these will enhance your sci-fi themed game experience while protecting your eyes.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Nexus Ops was one of my first purchases after deciding to get into the hobby. I've played the game a handful of times and while it's solid, it never took off for my group. A large part of this is due to the random hex distribution. If one player (or team as in our last game) gets high value mines close to their home, it's extremely hard and frustrating to overcome.

It also seems we can't go a single game without discovering a couple turns later that a unit entered an illegal spot or is illegally running a mine. Repeated plays would eventually cement this in our heads, but the game has a couple of these rules that are easily messed up in the heat of battle

All said and done, with so many other great area-control conflict games out there, Nexus Ops is never a first choice for my group.

Update after two years and 10 plays: 8-->9. Rarely have I seen such a good set of rules.

RATING: 9
I didn't really know what to expect from this game when I opened it, but after just one play I can already tell this is going to be played many more times.  It reminds me a lot of games like Command & Conquer for PC strategy games, but in a board game format.  Basically you take over terrain and mine it it to earn funds.  With your funds you can build an army and take over more terrain.  All the players are trying to do this, so eventually you'll get into some excellent battles.  The winning mechanism of victory points for completing special missions drawn from the cards or pummelling opponents makes this game a wonderful challenge and a lot of fun!

RATING: 8.5

RATING: 7
It's a blast!  I really like the colors and artwork on the tiles and board hexes.  The box art is one of my favorites of all time- great color.  The minis have nice shapes and color as well.  It's simple and a nice mix of fighting, exploring and card play. It's really well designed and come from behind victories are possible.  I keep trying to play it by gaining territory and spawning huge armies (wargame fashion) but my son beats me by satisfying the mission cards.  Pay attention to the cards! Game length is just right as well.  Good, light family battle game.  Clear rules with no headaches!  I love the way losers of a battle get free power-up cards, and the game self balances.  It looks much better in person that in photos on the geek.  It's unique and one of a kind, a keeper for my collection.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
From a grognards point of view this game just lacks something. Think of it as 1 part Twilight Imperium and 3 parts Settlers of Catan. It's a SF tile game with an arbitrary victory level. Instead of various resources, there are various builds. However, just when things get interesting the game ends because someone has been declared the winner. Victory Points are earned for various things, many generated by cards, which can't be cancelled. So victory has not a lot to do with the tactical situation at the end of the game. In fact, an alliance of players could have turned back the leader and even pushed him to the edge of elimination, but as he accumulated VP early on he could still be declared the victor by the play of one last card.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Nexus Ops is a straight-forward, simple dice-chucker battle game (almost in the style of Risk) but has some elements of Axis & Allies as well (extremely simplified, of course). Beautiful looking game! The AH edition is gorgeous in regular light and even more mysterious in black-light! The game play is pleasant as well. It loses a few points for having a somewhat random and imbalanced mission completion mechanic.  You can end up with some crappy missions to complete while your opponent has a few strong ones that are easy to complete.  Some of the rules regarding card play also feel like they weren't really well-considered before publication.  That being said, I like Nexus Ops for being incredibly atmospheric and it has several things in it which are perfectly fine for this sort of game.  I just wish the Missions were a little more balanced.

RATING: 5
This is a high-conflict game, which isn't usually my style, but it works very well. There is a very short lead time as players build up forces, and then you're into the struggle to earn VPs in battle. It moves quickly, and there are no complicated rules. The "VPs for specific objectives" mechanism requires players to attack each other, but doesn't require them to crush the weakest player, so even if you're losing it's not necessarily in the other players' interests to crush you.

RATING: 6
For what it is, Nexus Ops does a good job.  If I am ever in the mood for a kill 'em all game, this or Monsters Menace America will get my nod (Titan aside).  Nothing exceptional, but the relatively short play time, and the well balanced units make this game enjoyable, though never to be thought of as anything too deep. The forced aggression is a nice touch, as many war games generate too much turtling.  Luck of the draw for mission cards can cause a slanted outcome, but the short playing time allows this to be ok - as it never pretends to be taken too seriously.

It's an interesting twist on multiplayer wargames in that your goals are, for the most part, short-term rather than long-term, so while it lacks that desirable "grand strategy" feel, the game doesn't get bogged down the way slugfests usually do. The "king of the hill" aspect is a nice extra element and it seems well-implemented--the bonus is worth fighting for but not so powerful that it makes a player unstoppable, and furthermore it ties up some of your stronger units which could otherwise be attacking and getting you VP. The only negative was that my one playing ended up taking somewhere between two and two and a half hours. Despite the fact that this isn't my preferred type of game, overall I enjoyed Nexus Ops and would be happy to play it every now and then. One other thing--ordinarily I am completely indifferent to miniatures, but even I have to admit that the translucent day-glo monsters are pretty neat.

RATING: 7.5
Fast and furious little tactical game. There's quite some luck (well, at least randomness, but it can be luck) in what objectives you get and which event cards you draw, some of them are pretty situational and help you win the game in some situations, while being pretty useless in others.

Nevertheless, the game rewards tactical planning and even strategical thinking, with the different hexes benefiting different kinds of troops, and the combat system is fittingly fast, and both fair and, again, highly random.

Ultimately, while it's hard to take the game seriously both due to the miniatures and due to all the randomness, it certainly can be a lot of fun.

Also, the game nicely accomodates 2-4 players easily. Works well with 3, too, because relocating troops is so easy that there rarely is a runaway leader that the two others, if cooperating explicitly or implicitly, can't slow him down.

RATING: 7.5
Based one one solo play alone, I still rate this very highly, and I'm so glad I got a hold of a copy (in any condition, but especially pristine!)

The game plays quickly, with Mining syndicates buying units and exploring spaces to mine and recruit and then harvesting Rubium for more capital to increase their forces.

But the game objectives force a dynamic attack to gain secret victory points, mostly from combat situations. Players can't be sure what will aid their opponents, and must move and attack as they can to increase their own chances of winning.

It's fast moving Ameritrash fun, and it's a dynamite game, with fantastic components.

RATING: 8
Picked this up at the new reduced price, and was very pleasantly surprised by this game. Small random board forces conflict, the pieces are hard for me to distinguish (legally blind) yet are still neat, and the game is more complex than it might first appear without delving into the feel of older wargames I've grown out of because I no longer want to spend half a day in chit hell. 

I would even call this game elegant - in its setup, crisp rules, very functional playmats, and pretty neat Energize/Secret Mission cards. Because you are playing for victory points and not really territory (you do need mines, but this isn't a typical territory-grab), it quickens the game pace and adds reasonable random fun.

I think of Nexus Ops as an intro to wargaming with cool bugs. I have really begun to hate playing wargames with egocentric paralysis of analysis odd people, and thankfully, this game will not appeal to those kind of gamers. Only drawback is that you are limited to a 4-player max, but a very pleasant surprise nonetheless.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I can't believe stores are trying to give this away! I got mine from Funagain for $13, and it was worth every penny. Many people call this Twilight Imperium Light. Your operations are driven by the secret mission cards, and since this is a race for victory points, turtling is not an option. The variable board changes making each game different. The cardboard for the board is a tad thin, but the neon-glow pieces are absolutely fantastic! Combat is a la Axis and Allies, but there is a order of battle where the more powerful pieces attack first, possibly taking out weaker pieces before they can counter-attack. Then there is the mysterious monolith, which dispenses "energizer" cards that can unpredictably, and significantly alter the course of battle. Lots of fun, and highly recommended wargaming fun within 90 minutes.

Update (08/11/09) 4 plays: Brilliant, brilliant, brilliant. The best of Twilight Imperium, Axis & Allies, and Settlers. Fast, brutal action, with nail-biting suspense as the players race to 12 VP. The monolith is key. The objective cards drive the game, compelling players to take great risks and sacrifices just to satisfy the cards, meaning that turtling isn't an option. If this kind of "feel" could be expanded to a 6P version, with a more galactic theme, this type of game would surely be the TI3-killer.

Update (06/18/10): Have managed to get several non-wargamers hooked on this game. I think it would make a great gateway to more complex wargames. But who am I kidding? This is wargaming perfection that is accessible to everybody... and the pieces are cool.

Update (07/24/12) 16 plays: Let's not beat around the bush--this is a GREAT game, easily bound to be one of the "classics". 3-4 years later, it is still highly regarded in my circle. The simplicity of the rules reminds me of the clean, straightforward rules of the classic Milton Bradley games, but with the intensity and sophistication of more current Euro/Ameritrash designs. Who thought you could pack this much fun, tactics, and strategy within 60-90 minutes?!?

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Usually Ameritrash overwhelms me with rules and dozens of poorly thought out subgames and decks of cards. Most wargames are simpler -- and shorter -- than most Ameritrash. But I like multiplayer smash-em-up games every now and then. Nexus Ops scratches that itch perfectly. It does a great job of providing balance via its "loser gets a card" mechanism and the hidden goals discourage turtling. I like the "one round of combat" -- gives a nicer feel than combat to the death; gives you a chance to bring in reinforcements and recover from a streak of bad rolls. An unlucky distribution of the exploration tiles can tilt the balance of favor (if the same person gets all the spiders, for instance) and players need to understand the necessity of early, aggressive expansion. Those are about the only downsides I've found so far.

RATING: 8

RATING: 8
This was pretty awesome, actually. It solves the problems of multiplayer wargames by putting a nearly impassible obstacle in the middle of the board, and avoids both dicefest burnout and hopeless slog issues by being quite short (in fact, its length is about perfect) and making the granularity of points such that playing cards is significant enough to help players catch up to those that may appear to have a large lead.  Components are unusual and interesting to look at and touch, the luck factor feels completely natural, and the decisions are important but not absolute.  This game is very engaging, lots of fun--and it is not stressful at all.  I had a great time. I'm also intrigued by the possibility of partnership play.(2 plays)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
First played May 29th, 2008. Taught in 15-20 min, played in about 2 hours. This has a good luck/strategy ratio, however it takes a little longer to play than I was expecting. The goal is to be the first to get 12 VPs. You get these by winning battles or fufilling secret mission cards. The starting player gets $8 and each following player gets $3 more than the previous. Board has hexes of rock, Liquifungus Forest, Magma(near center), and Monolith(center). Each player has three hexes as a home base where units are placed and have a fixed income if occupied of $2, $3, and $2. Each hex unexplored has either a free spider to join your side, a $1 income and a (Crystalline or Fungoid), or $2 income. Units are(cost/die roll): Humans(2/6), Fungoids(3/5), Crystalline(3/5), Rock Striders(5/4), Lava Leapers(8/3), Rubium Dragon(12/2). Units can only move one space nomally. Striders can move twice if they move through or onto a rock hex. On your turn you buy units, move units, reveal unexplored tiles, battle, collect money(all occupied mines), and get a secret mission card. If you control the Monolith, you get two Energize cards(give special abilities). During battle, units are rolled from top cost down and enemies are removed before the next round of units goes. If the battle is decided you can play a mission card with a red VP value if applicable, otherwise take a blue 1VP. You may also play any number of white VP cards.

RATING: 7
Fantasy Wargame: dice:varaible powers:modular board:secret missions

A nice little war game in a reasonable amount of time (for a war-game). A little exploration added in the mix along with a modular board and plenty of cards should keep this interesting for a bit. Light on strategy which is pretty straight forward and dictated by your cards but the game importantly doesn't forget to be fun. 

Lots of interaction as can be expected and it is encouraged by the system as it is the only way to get VP's, also "the king of the hill" bonuses to be had encourage fights too. A good dose of luck in the dice as usual but even more so in the missions which is where you get your points from, these come from a random draw at the end of your turn but at least you have several to choose from. I did like the balancing effect of losing wars which gave you cards amounting to certain "power ups" to help you along the way. 

I thought with 4 it ran just a tad long (easily fixed by changing VP total) and just a little downtime was there but no real AP (maybe I am too used to 2 player war games), 3 would be perfect and apparently 2 works as well. 

I don't see much comparison to A&A or Risk, besides being a war game that uses dice it is quite a  different game and stands on its own fine. A good and shorter ALTERNATIVE to those games? I would say yes this is a relevant statement.

RATING: 7
Relatively simple area control game as you fight for control of resources on a planet using some of the natives to fight for you as well as your own forces.

Once you get into the swing of the game it plays very quickly and even though there's dice rolls in the combat there's a good amount of options and things you can do to attempt to mitigate those rolls and change things up.

You get points for winning battles, completing secret objectives, and controlling the center tile of the map. For the first game I recommend using the shorter game option where you play until 10 points rather than 12 as it goes a bit faster and doesn't wind down as much.

RATING: 6
2013-11-07

This comment is for the Avalon Hill edition.

Initial Rating: 6.0 (November 2013)

Good game, it is reminiscent of Risk, pure Amerithrash with lots of luck and many “take that” moments. I enjoy the game but lately I enjoy less and less the games in which you have to attack the leader.

The game components are superb, the miniatures look gorgeous on the board and they are very detailed. I want to have the game just for the components. I also like the unit variety (powers, cost, etc.), it  makes for some fun choices. The game takes around 2 hours to finish, which is very quick for this kind of game.

It has lots of luck, controllable in the form of dice, uncontrollable in the form of cards (secret objectives and recharge cards), so this is mostly a chaos game. I would like the secret missions to be less random but I don´t know how to fix this. I like that you score multiple VPs in a single turn so no one is really sure who is winning, making harder to “attack the leader”. Love the SciFi theme.

Bottom line, Nexus Ops is a good game I will play from time to time. For me, this fires Axis and Allies and most risk variants out there. I think this would be a 7.0 for me if played in teams rather than free for all, still it has the potential to go up in my ratings. I would never play it with 3 players, too unbalanced.

Current Rating: 6.0

RATING: 10
Space battalion light. My troops (of various races and powers) vs your troops (of the same races and powers) explore a region starting from opposite sides, looking for mineral resources (which become cash to buy more troops). Victory through combat only.

I tried to hand a Dragon to my wife and she went "eewwwwww... stop bugging me". And she wasn't talking about the New Nexus Ops smell, which I gather is a universal experience.

Still she kindly consented to a game. And brought tongs because she didn't want to handle the pieces! (I banned the metal tongs. She was mostly ok handling the bits by the end of the game.)

All that aside, this game seems [i]fun[/i]. Not just that the mission cards and simple combat system seem chaotic and light hearted, but ...

"My dragon is moving in to attack your dragons, and bringing 4 hit points with him. (ie 4 humans.)"

"Come, my Crstaline brothers, for glory and death and a bonus in our paycheck let's swarm the enemy!"

"Look, Thog, you stay here and pin down these two dragons while we retreat to get help."

"Suck plutonium death, alien scum!"
"Hey, we're the same [i]species[/i]!"
"Ah, sorry... Suck plutonium death, corporate weasels!"

I won that first game, but my wife finished the game occupying most of the board! "Ok men, we may have had the living bettlejuice beaten out of us, we may have lost three quarters of our forces, but we... flew in a suicidal dragon to their home base, managed to kill one of their dragons in combat, had a human survive a battle (gosh!), and when we used to have Rock Striders on our side they managed to kill a crystaline for us. We sure showed them!"

:devil:

The miniatures are pretty good, though more mini than I was expecting, but the rest of the production has a few quicky poor quality aspects.

RATING: 8
Not only is this a very fun kill-fest, but it taught me why I don't like most other combat-oriented games.  In Nexus Ops, you can be aggressive without consequences, without having to worry that you'll be eliminated or reduced to nearly no resources.  If that happens, another player's probably about to win anyway.  Turns out I like the aggression, I just don't like having to worry about losing territory I've built up.  Here there's just aggression, and waves and waves of troops.  Everything to gain, nothing to lose.

RATING: 8
Only played this two-player so far and if you don't mind confrontation, it's a great game for two.  It has confrontation without elimination, so it scratches an unique itch (I describe it as a cross between the point gathering of Settlers with the battling of Risk, for newbies).  And really, once you get over the buying units math, it's a great game that looks to scale well between 2 to 4 players (hopefully I'll get to play it with more).

There is a great balancing nature to the game, and the way it encourages confrontation as a victory condition without being overly punishing to anyone is simply fantastic (no elimination because victory is based on a point total).

So much fun that even though I've been skunked in each play, I still want to play again.  Shameful that this hasn't been played more.

RATING: 5
This game started off as a 9 for me and my gaming group but has slipped to a 6 and will probably stop at a 5.  No-one buys Dragons anymore as they're too expensive, fragile and need support or they won't survive long.  As a group we want to battle each other rather than play to missions.  The missions themselves are unimaginative - eg score 2 pts for destroying a rock strider in battle, but why?  The game also takes overly long to play to completion.  We often abandon gameplay after 2 hours and agree on a winner.  All units are the same for all sides and strategy is the same most games.  This game badly needs expansions to give it legs.  It could be a really great game.  Apparently Avalon Hill doesn't believe in expansions - they're idiots.  Their games are suffering because of it.  Especially this one.  Look at how well Descent, Battlelore, M44, to name a few, are doing because of expansions.  We aren't really playing this anymore, it's a pity.  Will probably sell on Ebay and buy an expansion for Battlelore.
UPDATE - just changed it to a 5 since writing the comments.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Number of players: 2-4

Components: (10)
I own the Fantasy Flight Games version and it comes with FFG standard componets. What does that mean? It means quaility! The cards are excellent, the artwork is beautiful, and the minis are on par with everything else. I understand that the AH version wasn't poor quality, either. The monolith was raised and the figures would glow under a blacklight.
  
Rules: (10)
I wonder if Fantasy Flight Games used the rules as written in the orignal print run, or if they came up with their own? The reason I wonder is because this rule book is well written. I LOVE Fantasy Flight Games. I think they produce and reproduce some of the best games in the entire industry. However, I also feel as if they go out of their way to have the most poorly written rule books. For the most part, it'd be easier for me to get a euro with rules written in German than to try to figure out what's being written in a FFG rule book. Anyway, this game doesn't suffer that way. The rules are great. 

Setup: (9)
There are a lot of pieces to sort out, but they all make sense. You'll need to build the board, also, but I think it consist of only a few pieces. Put some chits on the board and you're pretty much ready to go.

Area required to play: (8)
I've ever only played this game 2 player, and it took up a little room. I guess it'd take up a little more with 3 or 4 players, but really not too much. You'll need an area in front of you to display cards and hold troops and Rubium (money/energy), but most things are confined to the board.
 
Fun: (10)
This is one of the most fun games I have ever played. You command your troops around the board to gain control of resource hotspots and knock the shit out of any one that comes within knockingtheshitoutof range.

Theme Integration / Mechanics: (10)
I understand why your human can kill my Rubium Dragon. I understand why your human will not kill my Rubium Dragon.

Replay Value: (10)
This game would be replayable even without the secret mission cards, but with them? I could play this game everyday and I do not think that I'd ever bore of it.

Storage: (8)
This is one of the first games that I ever seperated from it's insert. It didn't take long to see that I couldn't fit everything back in the box in a logical way with that insert in the way. So I trashed it and bagged everything up. It looks a mess when the box is opened, but everything is organized well enough.

Accessibility: (10)
I think that this is a game that would be easy to teach. The theme and the miniatures will draw people in who may be hesitant to play games otherwise.

Over-All/Bonuses/Biases: (+10)
This is one of Jessica's favorites, and one of mine too. It's a lot of fun, it's very replayable, and it has enough of that "screw you!" feeling to really make us love it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Nexus Ops plays like a real time strategy game like Starcraft, if that game were a turn-based tabletop game. The gameplay feels tight, and while there is some obvious luck-of-the-dice combat involved, it is not overbearing. Nexus Ops rewards aggressive play, clever tactics and an smart resource management.

I don't really have any experience with dice-rolling combat games (never played Risk or Axis & Allies), so this should fit that niche just fine whenever I'm feeling in the mood.

RATING: 9
This game is just immensely fun. But that's such a minor quibble for a game that plays like Risk did when you were 12, only maybe even more interesting, and is just as good with 4 or 3 as with 2.

RATING: 7

There is an element of exploration and resource management, but in general it comes down to deciding which battles to fight in order to get the victory points necessary to win. The board (a series of hexagonal tiles you assemble) and bits (especially the colorful little plastic unit figures) are very attractive and easy to differentiate. Game play is simple and fast-paced, with a payoff that is appropriate for the game length and overall investment.  Well-designed game that is a lot of fun to play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Some people call Nexus Ops "Risk on steroids", being both games where you gather troops and where dice is the primary mechanism. While this is partially true, it would be more correct to say that its "A refined and polished version of Risk on steroids", as it picks what makes Risk attractive but puts in inside a much more nice package.

Basic rules are simple: each turn you produce units, move them around the board, battle your opponents, earn credits for the mines you control and then draw objective cards. Combat is balanced despite the dice element, as the random element they introduced can be compensated and controlled by effectively combining the different units. 

The game is objective-based, which makes each combat count: you cannot simply throw your troops at the grinder, but rather have to prepare your strikes to score as much objectives as possible. While you cannot score all objectives in one single attack, there is no limit of objectives per turn, so its common for players to score more than half of the victory points required for victory in a single turn. Being completely random, objectives are also what deprive the game of depth: certain objective combinations can be either very easy or very hard to achieve. Also, as you keep drawing them throughout the whole game, you can't plan ahead right from the start.

The components are excellent, even if I didn't liked them at first sight, particularly due to the strong plastic smell. Thankfully, it wears of significantly.

Nexus Ops is definitely a step up from Risk, not just a beefed up version. It's a pity objectives are so random.

Rating: :star::star::star::nostar::nostar:
Optimal number of players:
:thumbsup:2,4

Nexus Ops is a light-medium science fiction war game. The game boasts a hexagonal board that is set up differently every time, as well as (in the Avalon Hill edition) cool "glow" miniatures and lots of combat. Players control competing futuristic corporations that battle each other for control of the moon's Rubium Ore. By winning battles and fulfilling Secret Missions, you can obtain victory points.

RATING: 8
A tightly-focused gem that anyone can come from behind to win, a refreshing change that sets this one apart from other conquest games. The secret missions also make this a great gateway game for kids; the missions provide concrete mini-objectives as well as a real opportunity to thrash their elders. With plasma breath, no less. Nexus Ops scales pretty well with fewer than the max number of players. However, it does play differently with 2 players versus 3 or 4 (more like your typical "conquer the world" game, although the Secret Mission cards go a long way to rectify this), and the game can be unbalanced if the mines are distributed too unevenly. The addition of more players opens up the possibility for diplomacy (admittedly of the thuggish gangland variety, very much in keeping with the "amoral corporate mercenary" theme) and therefore more avenues for victory.

RATING: 5
There are many good points to this game: easy to learn, modular board, objectives cards, point based victory. The problem is that it's just a boring war game. I played a 3 player game. What generally happens is that each player has their territory and nobody necessarily insist on invading other people territory because they don't need to. They just need to fulfill victory cards. So what happen is that you have these little skirmishes at the border of your territory and that's it you end up losing or winning. 

Sure, it allows the game to end, but it removes the basic concept of war which is, you fight for some ressources or something. I thought during the game that my opponent would have taken his troops from the monolith up to my home base. But, there is no way he could screw me up because losing my base does not make me lose anything, I can still produce in my base hex if it is invaded. So why bother invading your opponent, just fullfill border skirmish and gain victory points.

Finally, the monolith is really too much powerfull. It gives you special cards which makes sure you keep superiority to stay in control the monolith.

RATING: 6
+ Simple enough rules (some complications about when cards can be played, though).
+ Nice game material (2nd edition). Nice miniatures. Clear player aids.
+ I liked strategizing about which zones to attack, which units to target, where to defend, etc. (but only for half the game [see below]).
- Hard to keep in mind all the secret objectives. 
- High variance in combats (single die per unit, and then if you lose in a combat with just a few units, the risk to have a strong unit be defeated)
-  Board situation which is not so fluid (at least with 2 players), and objectives that are variations on a theme (kill unit X, have a majority of Y,…): during the second half of my first game, I was realizing that I had to grind through the rest of the game doing the same thing over and over (because the goals do not have much flavor, so they look samey).

RATING: 8
Great light wargame. I had issues with the A&A system at first, my experiences of really long and not-so-good games of A&A made me a little bit skeptic. The game is awesome, the balance in it is almost magic, the only thing that really does unbalance the game is if someone gets 2-3 "2" mines in the first turns. After a few turns the decisions are still simple and not really long-termed, what keeps it really light. The "objective system" keeps the game moving, you have lots of opportunities to win, even if you're not a "leader" (wich I realize is really rare, you have so much going on that is really difficult to keep hold on your territories, even if your opponents are not playing "right"). Great game, I'm really thinking of getting a second copy to play it with 8 people or play a super-large game...

My friends keep asking for it, it has become almost a staple in all our game nights, whenever we have 4 players we want to play it. 

[Played only with 4]

RATING: 7
Although we hadn't had much luck with wargames, I picked up [GAMEID=15363] when I saw it on sale since it had been discontinued.  I'm a closet sci-fi geek, so this was right up my alley.  The glow in the dark pieces looked cool, and the game looked like a fun dice fest.  I'm sure I would have loved this game as a kid as a replacement for [GAMEID=181].

My girlfriend was skeptical, but we gave it a few shots.  Surprisingly, she thought it was decent, and more interesting than [GAMEID=10630].  I think part of the reason is that there is a small economic component to the game rather than just hurling armies and dice at each other.  There are hidden tiles that you have to explore.  There are strategic mines where you harvest resources to upgrade your armies.  There are strategic positions like the central Monolith that you fight for.  There are also secret missions that you try to accomplish for bonuses.  And if you lose a battle, you get a bonus Energize power card that helps you out by giving you special abilities.  Moreover, taking a page out of many Euro games, you don't necessarily have to obliterate your enemy to win the game---you just have to collect enough Victory Points through other goals.

However, the two player game didn't work as well for us, as we observed a strong runaway leader problem.  Once you fall behind in resources or lose control of the Monolith, it becomes difficult to claw your way back.  The Energize cards try to provide a balancing mechanism to the losing player, but the one of the big issues we've found is that whoever controls the central Monolith (and it's usually the winning player) gets two Energize cards as well, so no real balancing actually occurs.  With more players, this effect would be less pronounced due to shifting player alliances and ganging up on the leader. 

Although I found [GAMEID=15363] fun and interesting, this game is probably much better with more people than with just two.  Unfortunately, we have yet to break it out with more players, but I'm looking forward to that day.

RATING: 10
Do we really need another Risk/Axis & Allies knockoff?  That question prevented me from trying out this game for a year or two.  After playing it, however, I found the answer is "Yes we do!"  Well, at least when it's the best of the Risk/A&A-style games, we do.  It's also the game that Twilight Imperium 3 should have been--much simpler and more playable without losing any of the fun.  I have to hand it to the new Avalon Hill for coming up with an excellent new (if derivative) game, even if they have decided to discontinue production of it :(.  This has quickly become a new favorite game.  It has a modular board, randomly determined and secret victory conditions, and it's quite possible to win the game even when two players gang up on you, so it works well with 3 players.  This game accomplishes what many others have set out to do, but came up short.  Destined to be a classic.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Game in a Nutshell: Compete on a modular board (planet) to gain victory points for completing battle oriented missions. Use the planets own creatures, each with they're own strengths to battle it out via stream-lined dice battles.

Played as: 2,3,4 player

Pros: It's just fun. Play this under a couple of black-lights and you have a gaming experience that is near impossible to top. Doesn't do too little or too much for what it is: The board is modular enough to add the right amount of variation from game to game while not being radically inconsistent, and a good variety of units with enough to distinguish themselves while not being overly complicated. The battles don't drag on like some other war-games, and it seems to work out that good decision making can give you a chance even when luck isn't on your side. Plus the game looks cool while you're playing it.

Con: A game like this, simple and fun, shouldn't take as much time as this usually does (~2 hours, though this may be because of the way we played it). Doesn't work with four people (free-for-all) as much as I'd like as the economy gets a little tight and I want more money for me... to buy more rubium dragons.

Other: I like to sell this game by telling people it's a combination of Risk and Settlers... in space. Played under a black-light, this game may approach a 10. Still wanting to try this game with teams. 

Best Use: When people feel like Risk, but in style and with a splash of Euro.

RATING: 6.5
Hits the sweet spot for light-beer-fun-I-slaughter-you game, while still having enough of strategical things to ponder about. Best played if people just attack, attack, attack - and then, when nobody expects it, make some strange move that gives you a shipload of points to set you up for the win a turn later......fun stuff.
Steer clear if you hate dice though, or if comments like "my dragons just toasted your pitiful humanoids" bother you in any way.

It is somewhat a pity that one burns out a bit on this game after a while because the mission cards get old and the unit types are always the same. It is even more a pity that Avalon Hill has already ditched this game which means that an expansion with some new missions, unit types, and terrain ideas, being more than welcome and actually adding something, is completely out of the question.

It's got just enough of everything (cool minis, variety of units, 2-, 3- and 4-player suitability, cruel card plays, short game play, simple but not simplistic rules, science fiction goodness) to please science fiction geeks, light wargame fans and AT devotees.

Well done! I'd like to see more games of this type. I especially like the fact that, although it has the look of a game that works best with four, it plays great with two and three. A very versatile and entertaining game.

This is one of my favorite games, and it's one of the few that I consider a must-play at conventions (along with Arkham, Rommel in the Desert and Duel of Ages.)

I'm bumping this to a 9, because its quality is persistent, and I've never had a bad game of it. I'm not sure if I can say that of any other game I've played more than five times.

Well, revel in the momentous occasion, fellow geeks. Behold, the first game I have elevated to a 10 rating. All my other 10s--from my formative years. But no longer can I justify holding this back. There's just nothing at all to ding it for. Welcome to PerfectHappyGameLand, Mr. Ops.

RATING: 6
Good production values with cool looking "gummy bear"/dayglo, candy like plastic pieces.  You'll really appreciate the art direction in the AH edition once you've seen the FFG edition...

This is a light conflict game which prods players into taking the offensive... turtling too much will lead to defeat (definitely a good thing in this type of game!).  Not deeply strategic, but there are tactical options and the game plays swiftly.

Some of the Secret Mission cards are unbalanced in that some objectives  are much easier to fulfill than others.  This negative is somewhat offset by the game's shortish playing time and a player's ability to discard Secret Mission cards for rubium.
s
Lots of random elements, which adds to the enjoyment if you're a Risk/Ameritrash fan.  Games can be finished in under 90 minutes with experienced players.

As it stands, Nexus Ops is a game that I'm still willing to play but no longer feel like I need to own, and in some respects, the game has been superceded by games like Kemet, Forbidden Stars and Cyclades.  Going to miss those dayglo game pieces though.

--------------------------------------------------------

The number of unit pieces per player, number/type of hex tiles, card mix (Energizer, Secret Mission) and exploration tokens remain the same.

Other differences between the two editions not mentioned above:

Standard Rules:
In the FFG edition, you can discard anytime during your turn, as many Secret Mission or Energize cards as you like, for 1 rubium per discarded card. Discarded cards are placed face up on the discard pile.

Secret Mission Card:
Death from Above
AH: Move at least 2 Rubium Dragons from the Monolith to opponent's level-3 mine space and win a battle.
FFG: Win a battle in an enemy home space with at least 1 Rubium Dragon moved from the Monolith.

Energize Cards:
Radiation Cloud
AH: Destroy 2 Humans.
FFG: Destroy 2 Humans in the same hex.

Scattered Rubium
AH: Gain 1 rubium for each space you control outside your home base.
FFG: Collect 3 rubium OR Collect 2 rubium for each of your controlled hexes that does not contain a refinery.

Frenzy
Clarification.
AH: Each unit gets 2 attacks instead of 1 this battle. Play before battle begins.
FFG: During this battle, each unit in this hex (BOTH friendly and enemy) rolls two attack dice instead of only one. Apply both results. Play this card before a battle begins.

Six Optional Exploration Tokens
+7 Rubium (2)
+2 Energizer Cards (2)
+1 Energizer Card and a level-1 mine (2)

Use the new tokens with the proviso that the total value of all mines available in the exploration token mix is at least 16 rubium.

RATING: 6
A miniature-based game wherein players buy units who can then go control board spaces and/or attack enemy units.  Points are earned by winning battles or accomplishing goals on secret "objective" cards.  

Unfortunately, players only draw 1 "objective" card per round.  This means that luck plays a huge part in who gets cards that are accomplishable (as some objectives require other players to have built certain units, etc.).  Additionally, units move very slowly, meaning that accomplishing objectives often takes multiple turns and responding to changing situations also takes multiple turns.  

The game ends immediately upon the first player reaching 12 points; this seems very sudden to me, and creates a race for that 12th point (even if it means leaving openings that other players could have exploited).  I'd rather have seen it end with a full round after one player hits 12 or some such (as people are often ramping up at the end).

RATING: 6
Excellent game. Perfect for scratching the dudes on a map area control itch without investing 4+ hours, but despite the short(er) length, it still manages to retain a fair bit of depth. Also, the secret objectives cards add another layer to the basic area control game. They make the game more interesting, help with replay value, and also help to shorten the game by guiding (but not dictating) your strategy, so you don't have to deal with AP-prone players agonizing over when and where to attack. The pacing of the game is also very fast, so there's not a ton of downtime, and it remains interesting even on other players' turns, even when you're not involved in any battles.

Update: Dropped rating to a 6. After a few plays of this, it seems like the game tends to play out too quickly and predictably, and luck of the draw on the cards is a slightly bigger factor than I previously estimated. I think I initially liked the idea of being able to get a quick fix of a dice combat game, but as it turns out, I think I'd prefer to either commit to a full-length wargame, or just play something else. The thing that draws me to dudes on a map games in the first place is the plotting, scheming, and maneuvering, and the ebb and flow of battles won and lost. This game is just too short, and too small, to be able to provide much of that.

RATING: 9
As owners of immortal galactic corporations it is your job to find new moons that will yield the valuable resource Rubium.

In Nexus Ops you have found just such a moon but so have the opposition. Now you must enslave various alien species in order to secure those riches for yourself.

Nexus Ops is one of the best conflict games on the market as it offers a variable board, equal balance for any number of players and some neat mechanics that encourage aggression (keeps the play time down) and variability (mission cards). 

A must for fans of the conflict genre.

This one remains okay, but not something I'm chomping at the bit to play anymore.
