=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A very good tactical/strategic battle game. So far we've just played one game so my views and ratings on the game may of course go up or down with further play... but so far so good. Nexus Ops is a relatively simple and fast moving game. Like many games... it takes a few turns to get a feel for the game play and learn the rules about what can and can't be done. There are a few niggly bits in the rulebook but nothing that can't be cleared up by reading the discussions of BGG. The rules generally are straight forward and clearly written but could perhaps show a few more examples for some of the action cards which have the effect of altering the game play depending on where and when they are used. I think the designer/publisher wanted a very concise rulebook and they actually could have made it a couple pages longer and cleared up a few issues people are having.

That aside though.. the game works smoothly at delivering a fast paced battle experience where you are constantly thinking about how to increase your forces the most effectively and which forces should be moving in what direction to best take advantage of the various terrain effects. I can't help but compare this game to Heroscape in that terrain effects work much better here in Nexus Ops than in HS. The variety of characters in Heroscape is certainly more diverse but the simplicity and "efficiency" of Nexus Ops actually is a strength. The game feels well balanced and there isn't a tonne of unneeded chrome making the game more complex than it has to be. I would say it's almost a perfect combination of a luck "influenced" battle game crossed with the balancing sensibilities of a Euro Game. The balance they have achieved produces an interesting game that goes on for just long enough so you aren't getting tired of the mechanisms by the end of it all. The ability to raise or lower the VP victory conditions is another nice feature as you can make the game as long or as short as you choose.

RATING: 6
Wow.  It's a real shame that it took so long for me to get this to the table, as this is a really great little game.  It doesn't try to be more than just a pure combat game, but it does that so well it doesn't matter.  And more than any other confrontation game, it really encourages expansion and confrontation.  If you sit back and turtle up, you won't win.

As with other games like this, it can be quite lucky.  This is compounded by the random distribution of the mines and other resources.  It is possible, especially with three players, that there might be a "bald" spot in the board, where no player wants to fight because no mines exist there.  As a result, the players may end up ganging up on one player.  Still, the game is short and sweet, and any flaws in the gameplay are minor.  This is the perfect game to let out that aggression and get in some good conflict.

RATING: 6
Gather resources to spend on one of 6 types of units (humans, fungoids, crystalline, rock striders, lava leapers, and dragons). Use units to occupy hexes to get more resorces. VPs awarded for winning battles and satisfying conditions on mission cards. Earn bonus cards by occupying a monolith in the center of the board or by losing a battle. Battles are decided by dice. There is a lot of luck, for instance, all the two value mines could show up on an opponent's side of the board, plus if someone goes first they could get to the monolith before you do and you may not be able to displace them. If you don't mind the luck, the figures are neat, and it plays quickly.

RATING: 9
Nexus Ops is an objective game. It is not area control, it is not a wargame. You are given objective cards which give you certain conditions to meet in order to obtain victory points. There is no ebb and flow of point like in an area control game, there is little overarching combat strategy/tactics like in a wargame.

On your turn, you simply build your units and try and meet some of your objectives. These can be controlling certain areas, eliminating certain units, winning battles in certain areas or performing some action. The key point is that once completed, these points cannot be taken from you. There will be a steady rise until the point where someone wins. You may obtain cards that give your units benefits during the game. These generally deal with improving either your income, or helping your units in a battle.

The shortness of this game is what appeals to me. It is a good combat objective game, and since it can play in under an hour, it doesn’t feel dragged out. It can almost be considered more of a race, since you’re usually just trying to meet your objectives before the other guy. You’re generally not playing to eliminate or hinder your opponents, although, this can sometimes help you towards victory.

RATING: 8
[b]Nexus Ops[/b] was reacquired* in a math trade (1/5/16)

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]:

[i]I actually regret selling this one; I actually forgot I had it listed in the marketplace and it sold months later... whoops. My original thoughts still stand and in the end I actually enjoyed it more than Kemet due to the simplicity of set up and teaching. I miss having a quick skirmish game in our collection, Memoir is great, but set up can be a bear.

I ended up getting a copy of the old Avalon Hill version since I like the minis better and it has the awesome center monolith. Also older daughter (now 10) loves Nexus Ops too.[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (8.5):[/COLOR]

[i]- Great replacement for Risk.
- Works really well as a 2p game.
- No player elimination, person to garner x victory points wins.
- The game encourages combat, that is how you get most of your VP.
- Fast set-up and gameplay, about 45 minutes to an hour.
- Beautiful minis for the units.
- Energize cards add to the replayability, especially combat.
- Alternate unit powers.
- Dice based combat is handled simply but allows for interesting decisions concerning which units to have fight.
- Set up of the board has some random elements which adds to the replay factor.

So far I have only played with my son so I am not sure how it will go over with the rest of the family. My older daughter wants to play and she could easily pick up the rules and understand the tactics of the game. The theme is fun, there is nothing gory about any of the artwork. I highly recommend this as a fun combat game to play with your kids.[/i]


*Was initially sold via marketplace 11/1/15 [i](this was the FF second edition, I reacquired the superior AH first edition).[/i]

Good game.  The original game has better art.

But being able to flip the unit card in the FFG game and have a whole new army adds some replay value and makes it a bit more interesting, although I'm not sure the alternates are balanced against the original units.

So the FFG edition is slightly worse and slightly better than the AH/Hasbro edition, depending upon what aspect of the game matters to you the most.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a marvelous dudes on the map game. The game has a nice variety of figures who have easy to understand abilities. The minis (in the original version) are fun looking and glow under blacklight. The points system is also very easy as points get awarded for combat, or playing the card to claim credit for having created a specific board state. The game is very clever in that it properly incentives you to spread your forces thin early on, to try to hold the risky center of the map, to attack and win, or to defend and lose.  

I don't think this game would work well with less than exactly 4, which is limiting. Also, sadly for me the game uses dice for combat, which can be frustrating at times, since the best laid plans can be foiled by luck. That being said, if I'm going to play a dudes on the map game with dice, this one is by far the most elegant. It has everything good from the heavier games and none of the extra rules weight and fluff.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:nostar: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::star::nostar: Raw enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.75
An interesting take on the 'Risk' genre. It's not about defending but clever timing of attacks and managing your troops and money at the same time. The game almost feels like boardgame with some take-that gameplay. However, despite the fact that it looks very tactical and intricate, it does sport a very high degree of randomness, many die rolls and random card powers. The game is short and light-hearted enough that I don't mind, but it is definitely more at home in the Ameritrash-division than Euro and I might not always be up for so much randomness in my games as the lack of control is really more apparent than in many other games that are similar. The bits and pieces are very nice, it's a shame that the cards and tiles are ugly, kind of cancels the nice miniatures to a degree. A good game, not for everyone.

= 2013-11-11

Played once with my brother and we both loved it. I know they're very different games, but I no longer have any desire to own Smallworld or to keep Summoner Wars after playing Nexus Ops.

I was the first player and managed to get and hold the monolith for the entire game. I also got really lucky and killed two of his Rubium Dragons in one battle (which also satisfied a secret agenda card). We played a short game and I ended up winning 10 to 8, but it really could have been anyone's game all the way to the end. Even after I killed his dragons and went on a rampage wiping out his units, he was still satisfying secret agenda cards on every turn and keeping up with me.

Fantastic game. We sat up late thinking of other fun tweaks you could do (single player, co-op, teams, etc) and based on how many variants are included in the rulebook, this is a good one for tweaking.

RATING: 6
An abstract war game, and that's my biggest problem with this game. Personally, if I'm playing a game with war game mechanics, it must be heavy with theme and chrome. 

War games typically have extreme high and low dramatic moments -- high moments are large, fiercely contested battles, and the low ones involve rebuilding units and maneuvering them. Nexus Ops is no exception. However, war games have themes and and a historical lushness that keep you entertained during these low moments -- Nexus Ops doesn't.

RATING: 8.5
Score is conditional - only if game is to be played by gamers of similar game approach. Not a good game for casual gamers or mixed groups. But if so... -
Nice balance of multiple strategies, changing based on your secret mission cards and board layout. Excellent BALANCED, aggressive wargame to scratch the itch in half the time other games do.
Nexus Ops has basically obsoleted Risk and its ilk, because it succeeds at preventing the turtling problem inherent in so many games of its kind. The aggression required in Nexus Ops prevents stalemate positions and by design it reduces the amount of diplomacy (i.e ganging up, lying, backstabbing) traditionally that drags down games like this.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
+speed
It felt like a good amount of time. Downtime was absolutely minimal with no insanely difficult decisions. Often players get swamped by the amount of choices whether it is what units to buy, how many of each, who to attack, etc. The VP cards help guide decision to make. They will say specific conditions where you meet to get a point. It makes decisions clearer and turns faster. Battles are quick with a few dice rolled and then your turn is over. The game I played must have lasted an hour or less. Compare that to a 4 hour game of Risk 2210!

+bits
The sculpts are colored very well and the designs are all different. The figures do not really seem to match a common theme but I do not really mind.

+energize cards
This is what puts Nexus Ops ahead of many other games. Even though the loser of a battle just lost a great deal of men, the energize cards can help him out a great deal. To balance the cards from being too powerful, they are useful only in certain situations, which also makes it fun (and possible) trying to come back.

+close game
I think it's nearly impossible to completely eliminate someone in this game. That plus the energize cards makes the game always closely matched, which makes sense for players who are behind to keep playing.

+VP cards
I love this system. You only win in the game when you get to a certain amount of points. It means that a player who has been dominated in battles the entire game can still win rather than feeling helpless. It makes battles meaningful and different rather than 10 turns fighting over the same hex. As mentioned the goals also lower downtime by having players focus their actions in specific area.

+unique stats
It's great how the units you buy are different in their own way. I like seeing different abilities than just "unit B costs more than unit A because it just does more damage than A." It makes the game more strategic and rewards great play.

-monolith
I felt drawing 2 energize cards a turn might be too much. Maybe 1 a turn would be better? It the game I play, one opponent got enough free rubium to build 2 dragons and who also won the game. My opinion on this is neutral.

-identical players
The game is awesome, but I would have loved to see some unique powers between players. Perhaps each could make a unique unit, have a unique deck of energize cards, etc. Not a big deal, but would be cool.

In essence, you got the sheer brutalness of a wargame, but you cut out the problems such as a long playtime and player elimination.

RATING: 7
Nexus Ops does a good job of combining a lot of existing board game mechanics and distilling them down to a simple playable form.  The Mission Cards do a good job of changing player strategy at every sitting.  The main problem is that the game can quickly degenerate into an arms race of who can put the most pieces on a given hex (completely counter to the bite sized combats suggested by the design). Overall fun and easily accessible to new gamers with a pretty tight rules system.

RATING: 8
09-12-2014. Rated after 1 play. Fun, 4X-ish light combat game. Turns are quickly, each unit moves 1 step (or depending on special abilities more). Then if multiple units are in one hex, combatis triggered. After combat is resolved, at the end of the turn, you get a special victory objective card that rewards killing a certain creature type or control a certain amount of terrain types..

During combat, the fastest/biggest units are resolved first and hit on the lowest numbers. All units of a type, from both players are resolved at the same time. For each hit, a unit is killed. You may chose to remove a unit that has already attacked (a more expensive unit) or a cheap unit that would still have a chance to attack.

If you win combat as an attacker, you get a VP. If you obtain a special objective from a card you got (ie. kill a strong creature or what have you) you can get multiple VPs. The first player with 12 VPs wins. Game is light, fast and fun. Good quality mini's but the artwork on the tiles is relatively busy.

RATING: 8
This game is filled with wonderful reasons to like it.

:d10-1: Awesome theme
:d10-2: Great, unique plastic figures
:d10-3: Units with variable powers that are implemented well. Each has a value and a purpose. There are no useless pieces.
:d10-4: Modular game board so the map is slightly different each game.
:d10-5: Hidden objectives.
:d10-6: Energize cards that shake up the battle and give players special one shot advantages. They are awarded in such a way as to help combat losers and hopefully prevent a runaway leader situation.
:d10-7: An exploration mechanic which further randomizes the board each play.
:d10-8: A relatively short playtime for 2-4 players.
:d10-9: Terrain that effects tactics and gameplay.
:d10-0: Option to play as all against all or 2 vs 2 with 4 players.
:D Really Fun!

Here's what's not to like:
:d6-1: The component quality of the monolith. Really? What a flimsy piece. What's that all about? Screams for a do it yourself improvement, which many have done.
:d6-2: Too bad the rubium tokens weren't instead represented as some kind of glass or plastic bead for flavor.

The unfortunate irony here is that because of the number of my gamer friends available, who are few and spread all over the country, this game has only been played a couple of times. In all the years I've had it it should have seen many more plays. I hope to remedy that in the future as this game is so [b]fun[/b] and worth keeping. So worth the $10 I paid for it when my FLGS was unloading it in the very early 2000's.

RATING: 8
Impressions after first play: A distillation of most of the cool things about DOAMs. What makes this flow at a steady, quick pace is the fact that everyone keeps getting new goals with no effort to it and that rubium is plentiful. What makes this work with three is that the goals of the game are detached from anyone specifically. You don't have to eliminate someone to win, you don't have to have the most land area. You just net points for objectives that target certain types of units in certain locations. It doesn't make thematic sense, but it's the only thing you have to forgive to enjoy the game. The dice, the cards, the alternate units, variants and random setup guarantee variety. Rubium, while plentiful, is also just right. No one gets too much and too far ahead.

Do this: http://boardgamegeek.com/image/1441118/nexus-ops?size=large

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The fast and furious play time and anti-turtling philosophy in Nexus Ops can make you very thankful for it, especially if you've played Risk, Axis & Allies or any of the similar "old classics" (euphemism for lose an entire day or two from your life). But this game is not satisfying at all, since the modular board just doesn't convey the grand scale of epic conquest a world map can, and you get rewarded for doing stupid things like starting battles in two places. The game always ends right when things start to get interesting, and rewards the player who draws lucky objectives and is best at bending over backwards to fulfill them, not the person who made the shrewdest maneuvers.

The one thing that bugs me the most is the hidden special mission objectives. It's just weird and chaotic to not know what your opponent is aiming for, and takes away from rolling dice and fighting big battles. I would have preferred it a lot more if Nexus Ops followed the variant Risk games out there in providing more types of combat cards (a.k.a. scrap the Special Missions and include more/cooler Energize cards and ways to get them). Hidden special powers are tolerable, but hidden special missions aren't, especially when they completely corrupt the spirit of world domination.

3+3
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
If I'm going to play a multiplayer conflict game I'd almost always prefer to play something big and epic, like Twilight Imperium, Starcraft or A Game of Thrones or whatever. But in the lightweight class, this game is hard to beat. It reduces the genre to its bare essences (build guys and kill your opponent's guys) in a way that is both highly functional and fun to play.

The game can be set up quickly, explained in ten minutes or less and played in under an hour. You score victory points almost exclusively by attacking your opponent, which is a very effective way to discourage lame turtling.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A simplified game of collecting resources, using them to buy troops, and then battling opponents with your new units. Tactics are a bit strange since every unit has one hit point, and every unit does one point of damage on a successful attack. The battle strength difference in units have to do with their probability of hitting, each getting higher (along with the cost of the unit) across the 6 unit types. Other differences mainly have to do with special attacks, movement, and mining resources.

Definitely a lot of randomness due to dice rolling to determine battle outcomes, random tile exploration early in the game, and random secret mission cards throughout the game (which are your main source of VP's other than winning battles).

Still, aside from all that randomness it has seemed to me that the person with the best tactics, choices, and select board control still wins the game. At under an hour playtime, its a great game to play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
It's not one of my favourite game types, the Risk genre. The advantages over Risk is that it is a bit prettier, you have different troop types that have differing costs and advantages/disadvantages mainly based on terrain, you have several mission and booster cards that mix it up a lot, and it is quicker. So, actually it is a lot better than Risk. But it still suffers from die rolls (fun for some, frustrating for others) which can easily make or break you and who you are start next to can have a great effect. The cards have a large effect, which was one complaint, but i did not find this an issue as you cycle through them quick enough and if you don't like them you can cash them in for a gold(that would be rubium).
Revising this rating up as enjoying playing with enthusiastic kids ;)

RATING: 8
Played multiple times with 2P and 3P. Despite my limited experience with Ameritrash-type games, Nexus Ops fills in a niche in my collection. The gameplay moves quickly and requires some actual strategy in choosing which units to buy and attack. Of course, there are huge swings in luck, especially in the initial exploration of tiles (revealing Rock Striders is usually a good thing as it allows the player to quickly gain control of the monolith) and the secret objective cards, which sometimes requires players to plan out peculiar strategies. I prefer the game with 3P over 2P as it forces battles earlier, although there will be a lot more negotiating involved. Once the game is in progress, the board looks pretty psychedelic with the day-glo pieces on the tiles.

RATING: 8
[11 plays] Nexus Ops is everything it aims to be - ultra cool looking, fast moving, easily understandable, beer & pretzels combat, and very fun. I enjoyed it very much. There is some depth here that will keep me coming back for more.

This is a great gateway wargame for kids. Something that needs to be emphasized to new players is that Nexus Ops is a race to 12 mission victories. This means that traditional wargame strategies of build up and maneuver still apply, but that no player can afford to fail to complete some sort of major or minor battle victories on every turn practicable without losing ground in the race to victory.

The bits alone are worth the $15 that you can easily find this for. This is a cleverly constructed game system. The monolith benefits, the mission cards, the energize cards, and the nifty combat system are all terrifically interesting ways to drive the play and motives of the players.

I think this is best as a 3 player game, as a dead front between 2 players in a 4 player contest will break the game.

Most recent play Jan '16.

RATING: 7.5
Acquired: August 2015 - BGG Auction
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Set in the future, players are 'corporations' trying to strip a fictious planet of a precious resource named Rubium. They recruit creatures and mine for 'money' in order to recruit more creatures.

The creatures are the bulk of the game: they are needed for battles which in turn give VPs towards victory.

In addition, secret missions give players VPs. First to 12 vp wins.

It's a dicefest with alot of agony and screaming. I liked it. It plays fairly fast and is totally unoriginal; yet it works. Yes, your victory or demise hinges on the die roll, but you improve the odds with energize cards or by using terrain to the advantage of the creature race.

Heck, humans are cheap, if you hate attacking with one leaper at 3+ to hit or one dragon with 2+ to hit, buy 8 or 12 humans and attack with the lot. You have 8 or 12 chances to roll a six. That's ALOT of dice. Get the energize card that gives you +2 to a roll and you're going to unleash a world of hurt...

This solves the problem of many tactical war games, that are either about all-out attacks, area domination or alliances. RATING: 10
Fantastic little smash-em-up resource acquisition game.  It's good to go into this with your teeth bared.  The game encourages some wily playing as you try to push forward to tackle objectives.  

This game does a fine job of negating the turtling strategy as you have to get out and get points to win.  There is a great consolation prize for getting your ass kicked as well, which softens the blow a bit. The game isn't hugely innovative, if you roll poorly you aren't going to win the game, but I find that it has been boiled down to its essence.  Just enough chrome to maintain the theme, but this system is clean and elegant.  Great fun.

Every time I play one of the other "punch each other player to your right/left" kind of game I always look back to this.  Why bother with games like Starcraft...this is just all out better.

I believe this is the greatest game of its genre, it has made games obsolete and I suspect will continue to dominate the field for years.

RATING: 9.5
I love this game. I had first rated it a 9 after 3-4 plays but the more I play it the more I love it. It's simple enough to teach someone in a few minutes, plays relatively quickly and works well with 2, 3 or 4 players. The random board and secret missions make every game seem fresh and the diversity in the 6 different units is just great. It also looks really great and attracts peoples attention when you play it in public (despite some bad card/map art in my opinion). It's a fairly luck based war game though. So anyone who isn't a fan of those types of games probably won't like this that much. I, on the other hand, can't get enough of it. I think this game is seriously underrated and I actually think if it had a better theme and nicer artwork (the box is hideous) it would get a lot more attention. I'd love to see this revised with new bits and artwork some day but I don't see it happening any time soon. One of my all-time favourites.

RATING: 6
This is the anti-Eurogame.  It's garish, horribly violent, involves throwing abound buckets of dice, and had a pretty large luck factor.  It's got the board of Settlers (the eyesore Mayfair edition)and the combat resolution of Axis and Allies.  It's  shamelessly ugly in an endearing sort of way.  Its rules are a hodgepodge almost every game idea of the last 10 years (If it only had bidding for variable player order it would hit them all).  With all of this against it, it should be a dog.  In actuality it's a very fun light wargame, and the art suits the theme.  The rules are clear and work well.

RATING: 8.5
For the longest time I avoided purchasing this game despite the rave reviews it received from the AT crowd.  I would look at it every now and then, but each time would be turned off by the "cheesy space creatures" imagery (I tend to prefer that my games of conflict have some sort of historical feel).  Eventually I found it cheap on eBay and finally caved in, and now I realize what everyone was raving about.  "Nexus Ops" truly does take all the best elements of the classic "plastic wars" games and distills them down into a simple, intense free-for-all, with a modular board, variable unit powers, and elements of exploration thrown in to boot.  And most games end up being fairly close.  Even when you are down a bit, one turn can really turn the tide, as was evidenced by the two-player game in which I was down by 6 VPs and was about to lose, only to achieve 8 points in one turn and win the game!  Of course, the fact that this sort or ridiculous swing is even possible is a bit of a negative point with this game.  Now my next purchase will have to be a black light.  Game design: 4.5/5  Personal enjoyment: 4/5  Total: 8.5/10

RATING: 6
Not my kind of game -- super-light wargame heavy on the luck-factor. (Dice, random draws, etc.)

It's designed to force fast and aggressive play, though, and that I can appreciate. You only score victory points by killing enemy units, mostly by winning battles; you have hidden objectives to encourage you to make tactically-poor moves to get those VP. The monolith in the centre of the board gives significant advantage to whichever player holds it, providing incentive to fight over it.

Best with 4 players, I reckon, to amplify the chaos. With fewer you might want to think about strategy, and that's not what this game is good at.

RATING: 7
This game seems to take all the good elements of Risk and ditch all the bad elements.  It features great bits, a touch of strategy, a modular board, and a nice playing time.  Simply put, the game is a lot of fun and you definitely should play it if you like direct conflict games.  I only wish they did a better job with the Monolith production.  The other complaint I have is that the goal cards can be rather random and strange at times (e.g., simply get into a battle with another X-type of creature).  Since when did just entering a battle, and then losing it, become a method of earning victory points?  Seems strange to me and eliminates the need to actually play to win battles.  Fortunately there don't seem to be many of these kinds of cards in the deck, but they show up enough to annoy me.

A real blast and a solid effort from the AH team.

RATING: 9.5
Very well designed, tense little game. It's not going to replace my big, long backstabbity strategy games, it's an excellent game for a normal afternoon or evening play. 

It's complexity level (a hair more than risk) makes it easy to teach and the lack of special rules make it easy to just jump right in. 

It's a balanced 3 player strategy game, a feat alone that should warrant a look from anyone remotely interested. 

The more I play, the more I'm impressed by the design, which really cuts out a lot of unnecessary chaff in favor of immediate killing and getting "right to it."

Is it as good as Twilight Imperium or Civilization? Nope. Is it better and faster than Risk? Yep.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Streamlined, fast playing, no-nonsense wargame.

Has everything needed: varied army units, discoveries, a basic economy, a varied but balanced map, unexpected trick cards and a mechanism to prevent excessive bashing on a player (the more you reach into his territory, the longer supply lines to your base become and the shorter his). Top it off with (open) victory point based win condition and all games will get tense at the end.

All in all, great game, glad I own. Go to wargame if we don't want to plough through a lot of rules and are just up for some quick and fun bashing. Too bad it only plays 4.

[APR 2017] Though this is a keeper for the 'fast light wargame' spot, basic tactic is down: put lots of humans on the map to dissuade invasion, explore fast and hope for lots of spiders with which to try and hold the central spot for as long as you can. Let the cards take you from there. Easy, fast and not that exciting if you can also play Chaos in the Old World or A Game of Thrones. These do take more time and are somewhat more complex, though. But in the 'kids' spot it has been trumped by Conquest of Planet Earth so the niche is 'wargaming with "new" gamers on a time-limit' only. Oh well, maybe we should just try out the backside of the units and see if that spices up things.

RATING: 6.5
I'm not happy with the production. I think the art of the tiles really sucks. Someone probably drew the 4 different terrain types in 4 hours. I guess I'm spoiled by some of the nice art in other games. Also, I'll probably need to find something else for the Monolith since I've assembeled it TWICE now and it's starting to show signs of not holding together right. I don't like the semi-transparent pieces since it makes it a harder to tell them apart when they're bunched together in a hex.
  
On the gameplay side, I think the game is decent. It's a step up from some other Risk-type of games. There's no difference between any of the colors (except for color) if someone is looking for that aspect in the game. I think that the victory points work well and discourage turtleing. Some of the energize cards can be extra powerful if used at a critical time, but nothing is completely unfair. Exploration tiles can fall down in a way that the big mines are closer and therefore easier to defend for one player. Terrain effects are nice since each type has a special effect on atleast one creature. The variety and special ability of the creatures makes it worth buying more than just one type. The Mololith causes a "king of the hill" side-game going on at the same time. Worth playing a few more times atleast.

RATING: 8
Nexus Ops is a light-medium science fiction war game. The game boasts a hexagonal board that is dealt differently every time, as well as (in the Avalon Hill edition) cool "glow" miniatures and lots of combat. Players control competing futuristic corporations that battle each other for control of the moon's Rubium Ore. By winning battles and fulfilling Secret Missions, you can obtain victory points.

Units are composed of various alien races and have stats not unlike those used in the Axis & Allies series. Combat is also performed in a similar way. Players who lose battles are compensated with Energize cards which grant them special powers later. Players can also obtain Energize cards by controlling the Monolith, a raised structure in the center of the grid. First person to reach the required number of victory points wins the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2.5
*This* is a well-themed game???  The terrains offer some of the most boring and pasted on effects I've seen in a long time...

But, hey, we're mercenaries, right?  Do we ever do things in the game like make contracts with different employers to gain different things?  Nope.  As far as I can tell, the fact that we're mercenaries is totally irrelevant to the game play; it's just pasted-on flavor text.

Ok, so our goal is to get these crystals that power weapons.  Do we ever do any mining?  Do we set up mines and have to divide our troops between taking over a new sector and protecting the minors over there?  Nope.

Ok, so these crystals that power weapons, can we use them -- losing VPs since we won't be delivering them to our Corporation masters -- to boost our military during combat?  That could have been a potentially interesting game decision, blowing up VPs here in hopes of gains there.

Nope, not an option.  Guess that these crystals have to be cut or polished or something before being used... Not that this oh-so-thematic setting is ever explained to us.

This is why I don't take Ameritrash criticism seriously.  The ATers simply don't apply their own standards to their own games.  Apparently, if you supply glow-in-the-dark miniatures, a game automatically drips with theme and no one is allowed to question whether it is, in fact, pasted on.

All that being said, the gameplay in Nexus Ops isn't that bad; just mediocre in a fairly bland way, in part because the units are not differentiated enough to make your force composition decisions interesting.

The cards add some options but are a bit too powerful.  As in most fixed length conquest games, you want to enter the final round in second or third place and encourage the others to gang up on the leader.  The best way to sandbag here is to acquire cards and not spend them until the final round. A smaller hand limit would have discouraged this.

Meh.

RATING: 7.2
This is exactly what a light-medium war game I have been looking for which is almost absent from Euroames. The plastic figures are all of good production quality. The combat resolution priority and hit value distribution are all good elements to make it a risk-management, and less a luck-driven game. The secret mission cards are good motives to win the game rather than traditional player elimination. The one red-secret-mission-card limit per victory combat is clever. No obvious flaws is found from this game at its intented scale. The game is also very well positioned as a new blend between Risk and Axis-and-Allies. It captures most good elements from both games, but avoid the bad elements wisely.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Best of the light wargames that I've played.  This is Ameritrash and Risk done right!

Pros:
* Excellent tension
* Full Risk experience in a much shorter timeframe
* Excellent balancing mechanism
* Hidden Mission cards make it hard to determine the leader so less ganging up on the leader.  All players also feel like they have a chance of winning which both enhances tension and also keeps all players interested in the game
* Excellent and varied choice of units
* Excellent hidden combat modifiers in the Energize cards (enhancing tension)
* Rules are quick and easy enough to teach to board game novices
* No player elimination
* Excellent pieces and production (except the monolith)
* Variable board
* Cheap (when in print)
* Scales well with 2-4 players (although 2 is the weakest of the bunch but still worth playing)

Cons:
* Still down to luck with the dice as any game of this category is but I think it's well mitigated and the best of this bunch.  The game is also short enough that it's easy enough to play a 2nd game to redeem a bad run.
* The monolith is not of the same quality standard as the rest of the game which is disappointing as it's the centre piece
* The exploration tile distribution can sometimes give a huge advantage to one player (e.g. if they get all the Rock Spiders allowing early and uncontested Monolith domination).

Conclusion:
If you can find it for sale at a reasonable price I have no hesitation recommending it.  I'm not a huge Ameritrash fan but I still really enjoy this game and it's worth having in my collection to play with those who are Ameritrash fans, new gamers, and even occasionally with a crew that is almost entirely Euro oriented.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Fun bits, good variation of creatures and abilities, rewards aggresive play.  This is a fun, quick war game.

RATING: N/A
Nexus Ops is a light-medium science fiction war game. The game boasts a hexagonal board that is dealt differently every time, as well as cool "glow" miniatures and lots of combat. Players control competing futuristic corporations that battle each other for control of the moon's Rubium Ore. By winning battles and fulfilling Secret Missions, you can obtain victory points.
\13\\13\
Units are composed of various alien races and have stats not unlike those used in the Axis & Allies series. Combat is also performed in a similar way. Players who lose battles are compensated with Energize cards which grant them special powers later. Players can also obtain Energize cards by controlling the Monolith, a raised structure in the center of the grid. First person to reach the required number of victory points wins the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Another of the "new" Avalon Hill games...and a very good one.

RATING: 10
This game is flat out awesome.  The thing that makes it work so much better than related games is that it absolutely forces you to be aggressive.  Turtlers will stand no chance, and it'll probably even be in your best interest to over-extend sometimes in the name of VP.

The mission system works better here than in any other game I've played.  The units are impeccably balanced and have interesting and relevant special abilities.  Energize cards that at first seem underpowered later reveal themselves to be super-potent in the right combo and situation.

It really is a shame there's almost no chance at expansions for this game (assumption based on the closeout prices it was available for everywhere when I bought it), because it's a great, great system.  Still, as a one-off game, it is the perfect fast, strategic, light wargame, and I suspect I'll be playing it for a long time.

[A huge thank you to George Murray.  I was missing a yellow rock strider and he generously sent me a replacement.  My favorite game is no longer incomplete!]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
So, when someone asks for the nickel-tour on Nexus Ops, I tell them: "It's like a microcosm of Risk that takes 60-90 minutes and has secret objectives."

It's my go-to replacement for that Risk-like, dudes-on-a-map experience, partly because it's so compact and so quick but also because it accommodates 2, 3 or 4 players with the same level of excellence and comes boxed with a multitude of ways to play.

There's nothing spectacularly original in it's fundamentals. You collect resources, buy various units (each with their own special abilities) and march them across the board to conquer your opponents. But it's the way that the game is executed that just cuts right to the fun. The map is small and the game forces conflict early. You want to conquer enemy troops for victory points and control key areas for extra resources and special cards.

And the special cards are one of the clever little things about the battle system here. See, if the attacker wins a battle, he can claim a victory point (first to 12 wins). But the loser gets to draw a card from a special deck. So losing a few battles just means you'll have an extra edge when it comes your turn to get back into the game.

You can also gain victory points for fulfilling special conditions outside of combat. And here's the cool part, this is based on which victory cards you've drawn and nobody knows what you might be holding onto. The result is that every player is constantly striking out to capture those points and it creates a chaotic brawl of army on army and players' control of the board ebbs and flows.

All of this in an easy-to-teach, 75-minute game that is full of constant battles and surprising twists and turns. Easily Top 10 material for me.

Nexus Ops is a light-medium science fiction war game. Players control competing futuristic corporations that battle each other for control of the moon's rubium ore. By winning battles, you can obtain victory points, although you'll earn more if you plan your battles to correspond with the Secret Mission Cards in your hand to earn bonuses.
Units are composed of various alien races. Combat is performed in a similar way to Axis & Allies. Players who lose battles are compensated with "Energize" cards which grant them special powers later.

RATING: 7.5
This is a cool dudes-on-a-map game that really nails it. Pretty much the only way to get VPs is to be aggressive and turtling is not rewarding at all. The random distribution of hexes and exploration tiles add some variability without going over the top too. I like how the different units have perks that suit them thematically as well. Has to be one of the best direct conflict games.

Not impressed with the FFG copy. The hexes look like cheap Windows 98 backgrounds with photos of toys for factories. The exploration counters are cluttered, we actually missed the reinforcements on the factory tiles. The energize cards look nice and the secret mission/battle VP cards are okay. The minis are obviously the best part.

Sold my copy. Group has it anyway and now being reprinted.

RATING: 8
Millennia later, the socialist pre-industrial civilization of Catan has evolved to a cycle of mass-homicidal corporatist exploitation of limited resources in space.

...Upgrading steadily.  First few plays I thought it was quality fun.  Several more I find myself wondering about strategy and whether the game design isn't perfect...

Also, my son's favourite game.  Which makes it a practical "9.5".

...[a year or so later] I believe this game is indeed practically perfect.  If you're looking for:

1) Territory control
2) Point-to-point movement
3) Heavy combat with simple but statistically rational mechanics
4) Less than 1 hour play

I can't imagine anything more effective.  This is the true spiritual heir to chess.

...Another year and another 1/2 point up in rating. "8.5" is my rating for Xiang Qi, and I'm a classicist, so this is hard for me to confess.  It's obviously in a different class of game than Xiang Qi, but it's not right to prevent affinity to that integer just because it's not 1,200 years old.  Nexus Ops is a truly great board game.

RATING: 7
A colorful explosion of oddly shaped and oddly smelling pieces. I'm not enough of a gamer to properly shine a light on any of its "Euro" elements, although there's a bit of random resource exploring (ala Catan), mixed with old fashioned monster on human dice fests. Explore, earn money, buy forces, march them forward, fight for territory, repeat. Oddly enough, the winner isn't who has the most money, it's (mostly) who wins battles, which occur kind of like Axis and Allies (the defender decides who gets hit) with a "big guys shoot first" theme. The random board setup keeps it fresh, as does the random layout of what's actually found on the spaces. I suppose an experienced player has an advantage since they know what Missions are in the deck, but since most mission are battle based, you'd do best to just try not to lose any. An odd, strangely refreshing game.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Nexus Ops is a clever game that uses the same sort of combat system as Axis & Allies (that is, money is collected each round to buy new units, and each unit rolls a die and scores a hit based on its effectiveness).  What's clever about it is that you win by winning battles, not by gaining territory or otherwise capturing an enemy capital.  What this means is that even someone who is "losing" in regards to raw troop strength and income can still win, which makes Nexus Ops exempt from "turtling" and momentum-based end-games because even someone who is not doing very well still has chances to score points.  The process whereby the loser of a battle gains an Energize card, which will give him some kind of bonus in the future, also helps to keep losing players in the game.  I don't see Nexus Ops ever reaching classic-status, but it's fun and solves a lot of the problems inherent in other games of its genre.  I've only played the 2 player game, and I expect the 4 player game to really improve the experience.
